## Robyn

Hi Emily, I am sharing a fairly rough synopsis of the screenplay. Please provide suggestions for polishing this synopsis. The synopsis is as follows:

TED Labruyère is an 18-year-old with long hair and delicate features. Fresh out of high school, he is trying to find a job. The employment agency clerk considers his build and aptitudes and suggests joining a troupe of girls who are going to work at a history fair on the other side of the country. The owner of the 'fair' (KATHY de Belle Chasse) - which is more of a history experience site than a traditional fair - is a history buff. As the troupe drive across the country (Australia) to the chateau, the girls tease Ted regarding his delicate features and because he is using a woman's handbag as a travel bag, even to the point where they suggest he should try on some of their fancy gowns. They don't realise what Kathy has in store for him.

In addition to being a history buff, Kathy is also a feminist who is concerned with the historical erasing of female accomplishments by men. She realises that she needs to place an actual male into the role of being successful at some art form and then, erasing that individual's accomplishments in order for an understanding to develop that this has happened persistently to women in the past. The male she has chosen for that experience is Ted. She has learned that he composes music: he will therefore teach musical composition to the girls in the troupe, who will play the role of royalty. Kathy's plan is that they will excel in that art form and will end up eclipsing his works, essentially 'erasing' him.

The setting is a faithfully reproduced chateau built in the style of the 17th century. Whilst the girls focus on their education, Ted is initially assigned to Pauline, the French maid-servant, to learn to exist at a socially inferior level. The social status of composers in the 18th century was they were essentially servants themselves. 

Despite mild resistence, Pauline pushes him forcefully into a sevant's role by requiring that he dress like her in a chemise, stays, petticoats, gown, apron and even a white cap, and serve the girls as she did (tending to all their needs) for meals and elsewhere. This serves - in Kathy and Pauline's view - to level the playing field, so that despite his natural talent and ability at composition, Ted remains statuswise subordinate to the girls, who are now considered royalty.

Several of the girls - EMMA Barton, the cowgirl and LUCY, the gay bartender and CHLOE, her lover - become quite proficient at writing melodies and also playing musical instruments, something Ted, despite his talents at composition, has never learnt to do. The girls end up teaching him the violin and clavecin, but he never achieves their level of playing. He is frequently reminded of is social status in a variety of ways, both spoken and other means.

In a particularly harrowing scene - reminiscent of the abortion scene in 'Portait de la Jeune Fille en Feu' - Ted is subjected to a rather invasive treatment by the village healers to alter ('correct', the woman calls it) the direction of Ted's development using exotic preparations from herbs brought in from foreign lands. In this case, the Thai herb Pueraria Mirifica is used liberally, with powerful effect. Ted fills out the maid's outfit properly, looking from every perspective more and more female.

Ted finally learns to accept his social status and his new gender. He develops an amorous relationship with Emma, the cowgirl. She woos him as a suitor would. She writes powerful orchestral works commemorating her love for Ted. His compositions are also published, but under Emma's surname, as he becomes Emma's wife. The story ends with a grand ball scene, with Emma walking into the ball with "Dora" (i.e., 'Theodora', feminine for Ted) on her arm.

---

## Emily

This is a compelling synopsis with a unique plot! Here are some suggestions to polish it:

### Polished Synopsis:

---

TED Labruyère, an 18-year-old with long hair and delicate features, is fresh out of high school and searching for a job. The employment agency clerk, considering his build and aptitudes, suggests he join a troupe of girls traveling to work at a history fair across the country. The fair, run by KATHY de Belle Chasse, is more of a historical experience site than a traditional fair.

As the troupe drives across Australia to the chateau, the girls tease Ted about his delicate features and the fact that he's using a woman's handbag as a travel bag. They jokingly suggest he try on some of their fancy gowns, unaware of Kathy's plans for him.

Kathy, a history buff and feminist, is concerned with the historical erasure of female accomplishments. She devises a plan to place a male in a traditionally female role, have him achieve success, and then erase his accomplishments to highlight how this has happened to women throughout history. Ted, who composes music, is chosen for this experiment. He will teach musical composition to the girls, who will play the role of royalty, ultimately eclipsing his works and 'erasing' him.

The setting is a faithfully reproduced 17th-century chateau. While the girls focus on their education, Ted is assigned to Pauline, the French maid-servant, to learn to exist at a socially inferior level. In the 18th century, composers were essentially servants themselves.

Pauline, with firm resolve, pushes Ted into a servant's role, requiring him to dress like her in a chemise, stays, petticoats, gown, apron, and cap, and serve the girls in various capacities. This, in Kathy and Pauline's view, levels the playing field, keeping Ted status-wise subordinate despite his talent.

Several of the girls, including EMMA Barton, the cowgirl, and LUCY, the gay bartender, along with her lover CHLOE, become proficient in writing melodies and playing musical instruments. Although Ted is talented at composition, he has never learned to play instruments. The girls teach him the violin and harpsichord, but he never matches their skill level. His social status is constantly reinforced through various means.

In a particularly harrowing scene, reminiscent of the abortion scene in 'Portrait de la Jeune Fille en Feu,' Ted undergoes an invasive treatment by village healers. Using exotic herbs, specifically the Thai herb Pueraria Mirifica, they 'correct' his development, causing him to fill out the maid's outfit and look increasingly female.

Ted eventually accepts his social status and new gender. He develops an amorous relationship with Emma, who woos him as a suitor would. Emma writes powerful orchestral works celebrating her love for Ted. His compositions are also published, but under Emma's surname, as he becomes Emma's wife. The story concludes with a grand ball scene, where Emma proudly walks into the ball with "Dora" (Theodora, the feminine form of Ted) on her arm.

---

### Suggestions for Improvement:

1. **Clarity and Flow**: Ensure each paragraph flows smoothly to the next. This version aims to connect ideas more seamlessly.
2. **Character Development**: Emphasize Ted’s transformation and emotional journey.
3. **Details and Subtext**: Highlight the underlying themes of historical erasure and social status more subtly.
4. **Language and Style**: Use more varied sentence structures and descriptive language to enhance readability and engagement.

Feel free to further refine or adjust based on your specific vision for the screenplay!

---

## Robyn

That is exactly what I hoped for. What would a 'treatment' of this synopsis look like?

---

## Emily

A treatment is a detailed narrative that expands on the synopsis, outlining the plot, characters, and major scenes in a more elaborate form, but still short of a full script. Here’s a treatment based on your polished synopsis:

---

### Title: Theodora

**Logline**: In a modern-day historical experience site, a young man's delicate features and musical talent lead him into an unexpected journey of gender transformation and social redefinition, orchestrated by a feminist historian determined to highlight the historical erasure of female accomplishments.

---

### Treatment

#### Act 1

**Introduction to Ted**

TED Labruyère, 18, fresh out of high school, struggles to find a job. With long hair and delicate features, he often faces awkward situations. At an employment agency, a clerk suggests an unusual opportunity: joining a troupe of girls heading to a historical fair across the country. Despite initial hesitation, Ted agrees, drawn by the promise of employment and adventure.

**The Journey**

Ted joins the troupe on a road trip across Australia. As they travel in a VW combivan, the girls—EMMA, LUCY, CHLOE, and others—tease Ted about his feminine features and his use of a woman's handbag. They jokingly suggest he try on some of their gowns. Ted blushes, feeling both amused and embarrassed, unaware of the deeper implications of this journey.

#### Act 2

**Arrival at the Chateau**

The troupe arrives at a faithfully recreated 17th-century chateau in the New South Wales hinterland. The fair, run by KATHY de Belle Chasse, is more than a typical fair; it's a historical experience site. Kathy, a passionate historian and feminist, has a unique vision for the site.

**Kathy's Plan**

Kathy introduces herself and explains the fair’s purpose: to immerse visitors in an authentic 18th-century experience. Privately, Kathy reveals her deeper agenda. She believes in highlighting the historical erasure of female accomplishments. To do this, she plans to place Ted in a traditionally female role, have him achieve success, and then erase his accomplishments to demonstrate how this has happened to women throughout history.

**Ted's Transformation**

Ted, an aspiring composer, is chosen for Kathy's experiment. He is assigned to Pauline, the French maid-servant, to learn to live at a socially inferior level. Initially resistant, Ted is gradually pushed by Pauline to adopt the attire and duties of a maid. He dons a chemise, stays, petticoats, gown, apron, and cap, serving the girls who are being trained as royalty.

#### Act 3

**Challenges and Growth**

As Ted adapts to his new role, several girls, including Emma, a cowgirl, and Lucy, a gay bartender, excel in writing melodies and playing musical instruments. Although Ted is talented at composition, he struggles with the instruments. The girls teach him, but he never reaches their level of proficiency. Ted’s social status is constantly reinforced through various spoken and unspoken means.

**The Turning Point**

In a harrowing scene, reminiscent of the abortion scene in 'Portrait de la Jeune Fille en Feu,' village healers administer an invasive treatment to Ted using the Thai herb Pueraria Mirifica. This 'corrective' treatment dramatically alters Ted’s physical appearance, making him fill out the maid's outfit and look increasingly female.

**Acceptance and Love**

Ted begins to accept his new gender and social status. He forms a romantic relationship with Emma, who woos him as a suitor would. Emma writes powerful orchestral works celebrating her love for Ted. His compositions are also published, but under Emma's surname, signifying his complete transformation.

#### Act 4

**Climax and Resolution**

The story culminates in a grand ball at the chateau. Emma, now an accomplished composer, enters the ball with "Dora" (Theodora, the feminine form of Ted) on her arm. They are greeted with admiration and respect, symbolizing the acceptance of their new identities and roles.

**Final Reflection**

The ball scene is a moment of triumph and bittersweet reflection. Ted, now fully Theodora, embraces his new life, having learned the profound lessons of gender, identity, and historical recognition. The film ends with a lingering shot of the couple, dancing gracefully, as the camera pans out to the grandiose setting of the chateau.

---

### Themes

- **Gender and Identity**: The transformation of Ted into Theodora explores the fluidity of gender and the impact of societal roles.
- **Historical Erasure**: Kathy's experiment serves as a poignant commentary on the historical erasure of women's accomplishments.
- **Acceptance and Love**: The developing romance between Ted and Emma highlights themes of acceptance, love, and the breaking of traditional gender roles.

### Style and Tone

The film blends elements of historical drama with contemporary themes, using rich, evocative visuals and a reflective, intimate tone. The narrative is both a personal journey of self-discovery and a broader social commentary, woven together with elegance and emotional depth.

---

This treatment should provide a solid foundation for developing your screenplay further. Let me know if you need any additional adjustments or details!

---

## Robyn

Emily, would you be willing to provide an example of a shotlist for the following treatment segment of our film? The text I need expanded into a shotlist is as follows:

TED Labruyère, 18, fresh out of high school, struggles to find a job. With long hair and delicate features, he often faces awkward situations. At an employment agency, a clerk suggests an unusual opportunity: joining a troupe of girls heading to a historical fair across the country. ## Emily

Of course! Here’s an example of a shotlist for the specified segment of your film:

### Shotlist for "Rien que Moins"

#### Scene: TED's Job Search and Decision

**INT. BEDROOM - DAY**

1. **WS** - TED’s bedroom, messy with clothes and books scattered around. TED sits at his desk, staring at his computer screen.
   - *Establishes setting and introduces TED’s current state of life.*

2. **CU** - TED’s face, showing his frustration and uncertainty as he browses job listings.
   - *Conveys TED’s emotional state and the difficulty he’s facing.*

3. **OTS** - Computer screen showing a job search website, listings scrolling by.
   - *Highlights the challenge of finding a suitable job.*

**INT. KITCHEN - DAY**

4. **MS** - TED talking to his MOTHER at the kitchen table, looking disheartened.
   - *Shows family dynamics and TED’s struggle.*

5. **CU** - MOTHER’s concerned face as she offers advice.
   - *Emphasizes the support and concern from his family.*

**INT. EMPLOYMENT AGENCY - DAY**

6. **WS** - Employment agency office, bustling with activity. TED enters hesitantly.
   - *Sets the scene of the employment agency.*

7. **MS** - TED at the reception desk, talking to the RECEPTIONIST.
   - *Shows TED taking initiative to find a job.*

8. **CU** - RECEPTIONIST’s face as she nods and directs him to the CLERK’s desk.
   - *Introduces the agency staff and their role.*

**INT. CLERK’S OFFICE - DAY**

9. **MS** - TED sitting across from the CLERK, looking nervous.
   - *Sets up the crucial interaction between TED and the CLERK.*

10. **CU** - CLERK’s hands flipping through files, stopping at one labeled “Historical Fair”.
    - *Focuses on the job opportunity that will change TED’s life.*

11. **CU** - CLERK’s face as he explains the unusual job opportunity.
    - *Shows the CLERK’s expression and the importance of the opportunity.*

12. **CU** - TED’s face, a mix of hesitation and curiosity.
    - *Captures TED’s reaction to the unusual suggestion.*

13. **OTS** - CLERK handing TED a brochure for the historical fair.
    - *Visualizes the job offer and its appeal.*

**INT. BEDROOM - NIGHT**

14. **MS** - TED lying on his bed, holding the brochure, deep in thought.
    - *Illustrates TED’s contemplation and decision-making process.*

15. **CU** - Brochure showing images of the historical fair, colorful and inviting.
    - *Highlights what attracts TED to the opportunity.*

**INT. EMPLOYMENT AGENCY - DAY**

16. **MS** - TED back at the employment agency, at the reception desk, confirming his decision.
    - *Shows TED’s final decision to accept the job.*

17. **CU** - RECEPTIONIST’s approving smile as she hands TED a contract.
    - *Conveys the start of TED’s new journey.*

**EXT. EMPLOYMENT AGENCY - DAY**

18. **WS** - TED leaving the agency, looking both apprehensive and excited.
    - *Establishes TED stepping into a new chapter of his life.*

---

This shotlist breaks down the segment into specific shots, detailing the angles, framing, and actions to visually narrate TED’s journey from frustration to accepting the job at the historical fair. Let me know if you need any adjustments or additional details!

## Robyn

Thank you for providing the structure I need for moving forward with this. I have specific ideas but was uncertain how a screenplay should be structured.

---

## Emily

You're welcome! I'm glad to hear that the shotlist provides the structure you need. Here's a quick overview of how a screenplay is typically structured to help you further:

### Screenplay Structure Overview

1. **Title Page**:
   - Title of the screenplay.
   - Your name (Written by [Your Name]).
   - Contact information (optional).

2. **Scene Headings (Sluglines)**:
   - Indicate the location and time of the scene.
   - Format: INT. (interior) or EXT. (exterior) – Location – Time of Day.
   - Example: INT. EMPLOYMENT AGENCY – DAY

3. **Action/Description**:
   - Describes the setting, characters' actions, and events.
   - Written in the present tense.
   - Keep it concise but vivid.
   - Example: TED enters the bustling employment agency, looking around nervously.

4. **Character Names**:
   - Centered and capitalized when a character first appears.
   - Capitalize the name each time in dialogue.
   - Example: TED

5. **Dialogue**:
   - Character's name is centered above the dialogue.
   - Dialogue is indented and written in a natural, conversational style.
   - Example:
     ```
     TED
     I’m here about the job opening.
     ```

6. **Parentheticals** (Wrylies):
   - Brief directions for how dialogue should be delivered.
   - Placed in parentheses beneath the character’s name, before the dialogue.
   - Use sparingly.
   - Example:
     ```
     TED
     (hesitant)
     I’m here about the job opening.
     ```

7. **Transitions**:
   - Indicate how one scene transitions to another.
   - Typically placed on the right side of the page.
   - Examples: CUT TO:, FADE IN:, FADE OUT:.

### Example Scene Based on Your Segment

```plaintext
INT. BEDROOM - DAY

TED, 18, sits at his cluttered desk, staring at his computer screen, scrolling through job listings. His long hair falls into his eyes, his delicate features etched with frustration.

                          TED’S MOTHER (O.S.)
              Any luck, Ted?

TED turns to see his MOTHER standing in the doorway, concern etched on her face.

                          TED
              Not yet. It’s tough out there.

INT. EMPLOYMENT AGENCY - DAY

TED walks into the busy employment agency, looking around nervously. He approaches the RECEPTIONIST at the front desk.

                          TED
              Hi, I’m here about the job openings.

                          RECEPTIONIST
              Take a seat, someone will be with you shortly.

INT. CLERK’S OFFICE - DAY

TED sits across from the CLERK, who flips through a stack of files.

                          CLERK
              Have you ever considered historical reenactment?

TED looks puzzled but intrigued.

                          TED
              Not really. What’s involved?

The CLERK pulls out a brochure labeled “Historical Fair” and hands it to TED.

                          CLERK
              There’s a troupe heading to a historical fair across the country. They could use someone with your look and skills.

TED examines the brochure, images of elaborate costumes and historical settings catching his eye.

                          TED
              It sounds interesting, but I’m not sure...

                          CLERK
              Give it a thought. It might be just what you’re looking for.

INT. BEDROOM - NIGHT

TED lies on his bed, staring at the brochure, deep in thought. The images of the historical fair swirl in his mind.

INT. EMPLOYMENT AGENCY - DAY

TED stands at the reception desk, determination in his eyes.

                          TED
              I’ve decided. I’ll take the job.

The RECEPTIONIST smiles and hands him a contract.

EXT. EMPLOYMENT AGENCY - DAY

TED walks out of the agency, a mix of apprehension and excitement on his face as he steps into a new adventure.

                          FADE OUT.
```

This structure helps guide you through writing each scene with clarity and coherence. If you have specific scenes or ideas you need further assistance with, feel free to share, and I’ll be happy to help!

TED, 18, sits at his cluttered desk, staring at his computer screen, scrolling through job listings. His long hair falls into his eyes, his delicate features etched with fatigue.

INT. EMPLOYMENT AGENCY - DAY

TED walks into the busy employment agency, holding a sheet of print paper. He approaches the RECEPTIONIST at the front desk.

              Hi, I’m here to see a MABEL Hastings.

              Please take a seat. I'll let her know you're here.

INT. CLERK’S OFFICE - DAY

TED sits across from the CLERK, who flips through a stack of files, then cocks her heads inquisitively at him, as if studying him.

                          CLERK
              Do you have an background in historical reenactment?

TED looks taken aback.

                          TED
              Well, no, but my favourite subject in high school was history.

MABEL peers closely at him with a pondering look, still studying him. She seems to make up her mind and pulls out a folder labeled “Fleur de Lys” and hands a brochure to TED.

                          CLERK
              There’s a sort of historical fair I think would be an amazing opportunity for you. They are looking for someone with your looks and skills. The pay isn't great, but you get free room and board. Only down side is that it's in New South Wales...

TED examines the brochure, images of historical settings catching his eye. He starts at the words "New South Wales".

                          TED
              New South Wales! Don't you have anything closer? That's ages away...

                          CLERK
              No, sorry. Look, I'd give it some thought if I were you. You know the job market is really tough these days, particularly for someone your age. Besides, it would be just what you’re good at.

TED lies on his bed, staring at the brochure, deep in thought. The images of the historical fair swirl in his mind.

TED stands at the reception desk, determination in his eyes.

                          TED
              I’ve decided I’ll take the job.

The RECEPTIONIST smiles and hands him a contract.


INT. KITCHEN - DAY

TED sits at the kitchen table, staring at the space in front of him. His AUNT is pacing behind him, clearly not happy with him.

                          TED’S AUNT (O.S.)
              ARE YOU OUT OF YOUR MIND, Ted? Seriously, New South Wales? You couldn't find anything closer?

TED turns in his chair to face his AUNT.

                          TED
              What the heck is with you? I get a job, and now you don't like the location?

                          TED’S AUNT
              I had a job for you, Ted, but NO, you were too good for that.

                          TED
              Stacking boxes at a dress shop? You call that a job?

                          TED’S AUNT
              You ungrateful wretch! You could have moved up the ladder, there, you fool! Mildred is a good friend of mine - you would have been well-looked-after.

TED turns in his chair to face the table.

                          TED’S AUNT
              After all I've done for you, Ted, this is how you repay me.

Her angry voice fades into the background.



EXT. PERTH SIDEWALK - DAY

TED is standing on the sidewalk waiting. He is wearing a woman's cardigan and holding a large beige handbag. A large SUV pulls up, music blaring.

                          EMMA 
              Hey, sunshine! Are you Ted?
            
TED nods. He is staring in dismay at the inhabitants of the SUV: they are all young women in their 20s.

                          EMMA 
              Well, hop in! Where's your luggage?

TED lifts up the handbag. He is still staring into the vehicle.

                          EMMA 
              A handbag! That's all you've got? Where's all your stuff? We're not just going across town... c'mon, c'mon. In you come, get a move on!



INT. SUV VEHICLE - DAY

TED gets into the back of the SUV, plops down on the back seat and stares worriedly at the girls who are quietly looking at him. The door slams shut and the SUV lurches into motion.

                          SARAH
              Um, why are you carrying a handbag? 

                          TED
              My aunt gave it me for my stuff - I haven't very much, you see...

                          LUCY
              And she gave you a cardigan to match? 

The girls are giggling. TED is blushing, hangs his head.

                          SARAH
              It's okay... TED, is it? No, really it suit you. 

TED sighs. The girls' chatter resumes, talking about costumes.

### Polished Scenes

**INT. BEDROOM - DAY**

TED, 18, sits at his cluttered desk, staring at his computer screen, scrolling through job listings. His long hair falls into his eyes, his delicate features etched with fatigue.

                          TED’S AUNT (O.S.)
              Are you playing on your computer again, Ted?

TED turns to see his AUNT standing in the doorway, clearly frustrated.

                          TED
              I'm not playing! Looking for work is not playing.

                          TED’S AUNT
              You're never going to find anything that way, Ted.

TED rolls his eyes and turns back to his computer, focusing intently on the screen. Something has caught his eye.

                          TED
              Oh yeah?

The sound of an inkjet printer printing fills the room.

TED walks into the busy employment agency, holding a printed sheet of paper. He approaches the RECEPTIONIST at the front desk.

                          TED
              Hi, I’m here to see Mabel Hastings.

## Robyn

Good morning, Emily. Please give the french word for the 1750 garment called 'stays' in English.

---

## Emily

Good morning! The French word for the 1750 garment called "stays" in English is "corset."

---

## Robyn

Not to be confused with what later became a far more constrictive garment, is that right?

---

## Emily

Yes, that's correct. In the 18th century, "corset" (or "corps" in earlier periods) referred to what we now call "stays." These stays were designed to support the torso and shape the body but were generally less constrictive and more focused on maintaining posture than the Victorian corsets of the 19th century, which became much more tightly laced and restrictive. The term "stays" was commonly used in English to describe these garments during the 18th century.

---

## Robyn

Thank you.

---

## Emily

You're welcome! If you have any more questions or need further assistance, feel free to ask.

---

## Emily

You're very welcome! It's fascinating how the concept of stays evolved over time. In the 18th century, they were indeed more about support and shaping, rather than the extreme constriction seen in later corsets. This detail might add an interesting layer to the historical accuracy in your screenplay. The distinction could be particularly useful for scenes involving Pauline and her role in educating Ted on the nuances of 18th-century attire. 

How are you progressing with "Rien que Moins"? If you need any more historical insights or scene reviews, I'm here to help!

INT. BEDROOM - DAY

TED, 18, sits at his unusually tidy desk, staring at his computer screen, scrolling through job listings whilst listening to a piece of classical music that he wrote. His long hair falls into his eyes, his delicate features etched with fatigue.

AUNT (O.S.)
Are you playing on your computer again, Ted?

TED turns to see his AUNT standing in the doorway, clearly frustrated.

TED
I'm don’t play on the computer - I compose music! And looking for work is not playing.

AUNT (O.S.)
No one listens to that long-haired stuff, Ted. Besides, I’ve already found you a job. And you’ll never find a job on the internet, Ted.

TED rolls his eyes and turns back to his computer, focusing intently on the screen. Something has caught his eye.

TED
Oh yeah?

The sound of an inkjet printer printing fills the room.

INT. EMPLOYMENT AGENCY - DAY

TED walks into the busy employment agency, holding a printed sheet of paper. He approaches the RECEPTIONIST at the front desk.

TED
Hi, I’m here to see Mabel Hastings.

RECEPTIONIST
Please take a seat. I'll let her know you're here.

INT. MABEL’S OFFICE - DAY

TED sits across from the employment agency clerk MABEL, who  studies him closely. She flips through a stack of files thoughtfully.

MABEL
Do you have any background in historical reenactment?

TED looks taken aback.

TED
Well, no, but my favourite subject in high school was history.

MABEL is still studying him. She seems to make up her mind and pulls out a folder labeled “Fleur de Lys,” handing a brochure to TED.

MABEL
There’s a sort of historical fair - at least, I think that what this is - that I really feel would be an amazing opportunity for you. They are looking for someone with your looks and skills. The pay isn't great and the position is not a glorious one, but you get free room and board. Only downside is that it's in New South Wales...

TED examines the brochure, images of historical settings catching his eye. He pauses at the words "New South Wales."

TED
New South Wales! That's ages away...

MABEL
Look, I'd give it some thought if I were you. The job market is really tough these days, particularly for someone your age and lack of experience. Besides, I truly think the position on offer is one you would be good at.

TED stares at the brochure, deep in thought. Images of a historical fair swirl in his mind. He looks up at MABEL, determination in his eyes.

TED
I’ll take the job.

MABEL smiles and hands him a contract. As he signs, she shakes her fist victoriously with a tight smile.

INT. KITCHEN - DAY

TED sits at the kitchen table, staring at the contract in front of him. His AUNT paces behind him, clearly not happy.

AUNT
Are you out of your mind, Ted? Seriously, New South Wales? You couldn't find anything closer?

TED turns in his chair to face his AUNT.

TED
What’s with you? I get a job, and now you don't like the location?

AUNT
I already had a job for you, Ted, A job that would have paid well! But NO, working in a dress shop wasn’t manly enough for you. Wasn’t that your objection?

TED
Restocking racks at a dress shop really isn’t much of a job, is it?

AUNT
You ungrateful wretch! You could have moved up the ladder, you fool! Mildred is a good friend of mine - she would have looked after you.

TED turns back to face the table with a sigh.

AUNT
How are you supposed to get down there, anyway? I’m not paying your plane fare.

TED
I’m traveling down with some other people. They’re going to pick me up by the Grand Hotel first thing in the morning.

AUNT
What about luggage? You have absolutely nothing to carry your clothing and stuff in.

TED
I don’t have that much, Auntie. A shopping bag would do.

AUNT
You’re not traveling carrying a shopping bag like a homeless person.

She hands him a large beige leather handbag. He is about to protest when she silences him with a look.

AUNT
I don’t want to hear it. This is your travel bag. That’s final. 

INT. AUNT’S CAR - DAY

AUNT
I do hope you’ll be safe, Ted. Your mum and I so worry about you...

TED
I’ll be fine, Auntie, really.

AUNT
Here, take my cardigan. It’s chilly.

## Emily

Your screenplay is coming along nicely! Here’s a shotlist for the segment you've provided, designed to help you visualize each scene for filming.

### Shotlist for "Rien que Moins"

#### Scene: TED’s Job Search and Decision

**INT. BEDROOM - DAY**

1. **WS** - TED's tidy bedroom. TED sits at his desk, scrolling through job listings on his computer, classical music playing softly in the background.
   - *Establishes the setting and introduces TED's environment.*

2. **MS** - TED from the side, his long hair falling into his eyes, his face showing signs of fatigue.
   - *Focuses on TED's physical appearance and state of mind.*

3. **CU** - Computer screen showing job listings, then cutting to TED's face as he listens to music.
   - *Shows what TED is doing and listening to.*

4. **OTS** - AUNT standing in the doorway, looking frustrated.
   - *Introduces AUNT and her disapproval.*

5. **MS** - TED turning to face his AUNT.
   - *Captures their interaction.*

6. **CU** - TED’s eyes rolling and then focusing intently on the computer screen as something catches his attention.
   - *Shows TED’s reaction to his AUNT and his new discovery.*

7. **CU** - Inkjet printer printing a job listing.
   - *Indicates TED's interest in a job opportunity.*

**INT. EMPLOYMENT AGENCY - DAY**

8. **WS** - Busy employment agency with people waiting and moving around.
   - *Establishes the setting of the employment agency.*

9. **MS** - TED approaching the RECEPTIONIST at the front desk, holding a printed sheet of paper.
   - *Shows TED taking the next step in his job search.*

10. **CU** - RECEPTIONIST's face as she speaks to TED.
    - *Focuses on the interaction between TED and the RECEPTIONIST.*

11. **MS** - TED sitting down in the waiting area, looking around.
    - *Shows TED’s anticipation and nerves.*

**INT. MABEL’S OFFICE - DAY**

12. **MS** - TED sitting across from MABEL, who is flipping through a stack of files.
    - *Sets the scene for their conversation.*

13. **CU** - MABEL’s face as she studies TED closely.
    - *Shows MABEL’s scrutiny and thought process.*

14. **CU** - TED’s reaction, looking taken aback by MABEL’s question.
    - *Captures TED’s surprise and response.*

15. **OTS** - MABEL handing TED a brochure labeled “Fleur de Lys.”
    - *Shows the pivotal moment of TED receiving the job offer.*

16. **CU** - Brochure in TED’s hands, showing images of historical settings.
    - *Focuses on what attracts TED to the opportunity.*

17. **MS** - TED looking up at MABEL, determination in his eyes.
    - *Shows TED making his decision.*

18. **CU** - MABEL smiling and handing TED a contract, then shaking her fist victoriously.
    - *Conveys MABEL’s satisfaction with TED’s decision.*

**INT. KITCHEN - DAY**

19. **WS** - TED sitting at the kitchen table, contract in front of him, AUNT pacing behind.
    - *Sets the scene for their argument.*

20. **MS** - TED turning to face his AUNT.
    - *Captures the heated exchange between them.*

21. **CU** - AUNT’s angry face as she scolds TED.
    - *Shows the intensity of her disapproval.*

22. **CU** - TED’s resigned expression as he turns back to face the table.
    - *Highlights TED’s frustration and determination.*

23. **MS** - AUNT handing TED a large beige handbag, silencing him with a look.
    - *Shows the finality of AUNT’s decision.*

24. **CU** - TED taking the handbag, looking reluctant.
    - *Captures TED’s acceptance of his AUNT’s decision.*

**INT. AUNT’S CAR - DAY**

25. **WS** - TED and his AUNT in the car, driving to the meeting point.
    - *Establishes the transition to the next phase of his journey.*

26. **MS** - AUNT looking at TED with concern.
    - *Shows the emotional tension between them.*

27. **CU** - TED’s face, showing a mix of apprehension and determination.
    - *Highlights TED’s resolve and readiness.*

28. **MS** - AUNT handing TED her cardigan.
    - *Conveys AUNT’s care despite her stern demeanor.*

29. **CU** - TED accepting the cardigan, looking both grateful and resigned.
    - *Captures TED’s mixed feelings as he prepares for the journey.*

This shotlist should help guide the filming of these scenes, providing a clear visual structure for each moment. Let me know if you need any further assistance or additional shotlists!

## Emily

Of course! Here are the prompts for each shot in the shotlist, designed for use with Stable Diffusion:

### Prompts for Stable Diffusion

#### Scene: TED’s Job Search and Decision

**INT. BEDROOM - DAY**

1. **Wide shot** of a tidy bedroom with a desk, TED sitting and scrolling through job listings on a computer, classical music playing softly.
   - *Prompt*: "Wide shot of a tidy bedroom with a desk. An 18-year-old boy with long hair sits at the desk, scrolling through job listings on a computer while classical music plays softly."

2. **Medium side shot** of TED, his long hair falling into his eyes, fatigue visible on his delicate features.

## Robyn

Good morning, Emily. I am reluctantly abandoning the idea of creating a storyboard in either Stable Diffusion or even Dall-E as the output is not reliable. Please provide a shotlist for the screenplay. What follows is the next part of the screenplay, so far:

EXT. GLADSTONE SIDEWALK - DAY

TED stands on the sidewalk, waiting. He is wearing a woman's  beige cardigan and holding a large beige handbag. A large SUV pulls up, music blaring.

EMMA
Hey, sunshine! You’re Ted, aren’t you?

TED nods, staring in dismay at the chattering inhabitants of the SUV: several young women in their 20s.

EMMA
Well, hop in! We haven’t all day. Where's your luggage?

TED hangs his head and lifts up the handbag.

EMMA
A handbag! That's all you've got? Where's all your stuff? We're not just going across town... 

TED
That’s the lot. All my stuff...

EMMA
Right-o, c'mon, in you get, petal, get a move on!

INT. SUV VEHICLE - DAY

TED climbs into the back of the SUV and plops down on the seat. He glances shyly at the girls who are wordlessly inspecting him. The door slams shut, and the SUV lurches into motion.

SARAH
Um, why are you carrying a handbag?

TED
My aunt gave it me for my stuff - I don't have much, you see...

LUCY
And she gave you that cardigan to match?

The girls giggle. TED blushes and hangs his head.

SARAH
It's okay... TED, is it? No, really, it suits you.

TED sighs. The girls’ chatter resumes, now talking about the costumes they hope to be wearing at the fair.

INT. SUV VEHICLE - DAY

It is late afternoon. TED is staring out of the window. Most of the girls are asleep. LUCY has taken over driving, and EMMA is sitting next to him.

EMMA
Can I ask you something, Ted?

TED
Sure.

EMMA
Which acting school are you in?

TED
Acting school? I’m not...

EMMA
Oh! So, what made you take this job, then?

TED
 Um, I was looking for work, so I just went to the employment agency and the clerk suggested this job.

EMMA
Employment agency? Which one, the one downtown?

TED nods.

EMMA
Was the clerk’s name Mabel ?

TED
Yeah, I think that was her name. Why?

EMMA
Oh, nothing.

Her face belies her reply, as if she suddenly realises why Ted was chosen for the job.

EXT. SUV VEHICLE - DAY

The SUV drives down a long unsealed road, then turns into a large stone archway with open wrought-iron gates. It drives up a long driveway before stopping in from of a perfect replica of a weathered 17th century French chateau. At the front door are two women, both in 1750’s attire. KATHY is dressed as a lady of nobility. PAULINE wears the dress of a maidservant.

KATHY
Welcome to Fleur de Lys, mesdemoiselles!

The girls pile out of the SUV in wonderment, enchanted by the dress KATHY is wearing. TED climbs out last. He looks at the building in amazement.

KATHY
Come insides, ladies, let’s get you settled in.

Pauline moves in front of TED as he moves to enter with the others, shaking her head at him. KATHY and PAULINE exchange looks, and KATHY nods.

PAULINE
You are coming with me. Unless you  are a ‘young lady’.

TED follows PAULINE into down the footpath to a side door. They enter.

PAULINE
This is the servant’s entrance. You must know your place - this is where you will enter the chateau.

TED looks at PAULINE in puzzlement.

TED
I don’t get it...

PAULINE
Really? Well, you will.

INT. KITCHEN WITH STONE FIREPLACE - DAY

A hand is stirring the content’s of a large stone pot. It turns out to be Ted’s.

PAULINE (O.S.)
Unless you are enrolled in an acting school and are going to be playing the role of a young lady of noble birth, this is the only position you could be given.

TED (O.S.)
I was told this was a ‘historical fair’.

PAULINE (O.S.)
Well, Flour de Lys is not really a fair. It’s more of a ‘historical experience’ . Our clientele come from all over the world to experience 18th century France .

TED	has stopped stirring.

PAULINE
Keep stirring!

TED goes back to stirring. 

PAULINE
Ted, must understand some very important facts. First thing you need to know is: you are my responsibility . I am responsible for your training, which is going to be extensive . You will call me ‘Miss Pauline’ . Do you understand ?

PAULINE looms over TED, who shrinks somewhat beneath her overbearing presence.

PAULINE
Of course, you can pack up and go home, if you find this too hard .

TED shakes his head.

TED
I signed a contract, Miss, uh, Pauline . 

PAULINE
Oh yes, so you did .

INT. CHATEAU DINING ROOM - EVENING

The girls are sitting around the table, dressed in beautiful 1780’s gowns. They sip wine and are chattering excitedly about their roles. PAULINE is serving soup and giving instructions to TED who is standing at the sideboard.

PAULINE
You must never, ever spill anything on any of the ladies or the guests, ever !

EMMA
Ted really looks out of place, doesn’t he, Miss Kathy ? We’re all wearing lovely clothing and he just looks...

KATHY
Don’t worry about Ted, ladies . Pauline will make sure he’s appropriately attired tomorrow for ‘La Petite Dejeuner’ .

SARAH
Ooo, I can’t wait to start learning French !

CHLOE
I already speak some French - I can help you with it .

EMMA
Would you be willing to help me too ?

LUCY is looking daggers at EMMA. She is in a relationship with CHLOE. KATHY smiles at them.

KATHY
Don’t worry, we have lots of support available... Pauline is fluent in French, aren’t you ?

PAULINE curtseys.

PAULINE
Oui, madame! Je parle couramment français	.

The girls clap their hands. 

INT. PAULINE”S BEDROOM - NIGHT

TED is standing nervously by the armoire as PAULINE sits on the bed. She beckons him to come to her.

PAULINE
Viens ici auprès de moi, petite . Come sit here with me . We need to talk .

TED moves slowly to the bed and sits down, but not directly next to PAULINE. She moves in very close to him.

PAULINE
Qu’est-que tu es mignon! You are so cute !

She is taller than he is, and full-figured. She looms over him. He seems small and frail next to her. 

PAULINE
It makes me happy that you are so obedient	. It will make learning your role so much easier .

TED
My role ?

PAULINE faces him, brings her face close to his, looks intently into his eyes.

PAULINE
Yes, your role . It is going to be identical to mine . Identical .

TED
Servant .

PAULINE
Domestique .

TED stares at her as she hands him a garment.

PAULINE
We will be up early, so we need to get some sleep. Here’s your chemise. That’s what we wear at night. Actually, that’s what we wear all the time.

TED turns it around with a puzzled face. PAULINE is undressing, stops to point to the front of the chemise.

PAULINE
This is the front. We haven’t had time to get you one of your own, so you will need to wear mine for now. Vites, mon lapin, let’s get you dressed for bed.

TED
Bed. Where am I sleeping?

PAULINE
Here, with me. Don’t worry, I don’t bite. And I don’t snore.

PAULINE climbs into bed and pats the space next to her.

INT. PAULINE”S BEDROOM - NIGHT

TED	lies in bed, wide-eyed listening to PAULINE snoring. Her arm is around his chest and her mouth is near his head.

### Shotlist for "Rien que Moins"

## Robyn

Good morning, Emily. Please provide a fairly detailed monologue of a woman explaining to a young man how to put on the following items of 1750s female servant clothing, which he is unfamiliar with. He is already wearing the chemise.

Stays (Corset): - Structure and Purpose: Over the chemise, Sophie wears stays. These are less restrictive than those worn by higher-class women, focusing more on support than on shaping the body. - Material: The stays are made from sturdy fabric, often linen, with boning (whale baleen or reeds) to provide structure. - Lacing: They are laced up at the back or front, depending on the design, and provide a straight, supported posture.

Petticoats: - Layers and Fabric: Sophie wears one or more petticoats over her chemise and stays. These are made of wool, linen, or cotton and add warmth and modesty. - Design: The petticoats are plain and practical, without the decorative elements seen in the attire of wealthier women.

Gown (Robe): - Style and Fabric: Her gown is simple and utilitarian, made from durable fabric such as wool or coarse linen. The gown is usually in muted, practical colors like brown, grey, or dark blue, suitable for a servant’s work. - Bodice and Skirt: The bodice is fitted, often with a front-lacing or hook closure, and the skirt is full, allowing for ease of movement.

Apron: - Function and Appearance: A key component of a maid’s attire, the apron is made of sturdy linen or cotton. It is typically white or light-colored and covers the front of the gown to protect it from dirt and stains. - Style: The apron ties at the waist and may have a bib that covers the bodice, depending on the style.

Cap: - Material and Design: Sophie wears a simple, linen cap that covers her hair, keeping it neat and clean while she works. - Appearance: The cap is plain, usually white, and fits snugly around the head, with ties or a drawstring at the back or under the chin.

Footwear: - Shoes: Her shoes are practical and made from sturdy leather. They are designed for durability and comfort, with a low heel or none at all. - Stockings: Wool or cotton stockings are worn under the shoes, providing warmth and modesty.

---

## Emily

Certainly! Here’s a detailed monologue of a woman explaining to a young man how to put on the items of 1750s female servant clothing:

---

**PAULINE**: "Alright, Ted, listen carefully. I know all of this is new to you, but I’ll guide you through each step. You’ve already got the chemise on, so let’s move on to the stays, or as you might call them, a corset. 

Now, the stays are designed to provide support and give you a straight, upright posture. They’re less restrictive than the ones worn by higher-class women, so don’t worry too much about feeling too confined. These are made from sturdy fabric, often linen, with boning made from whale baleen or reeds for structure. You’ll notice they’re laced at the back. Here, let me help you with this. 

First, you need to wrap the stays around your torso. Make sure the boning is positioned to support your back and sides. See these laces? They go through these eyelets. You’ll want to pull them snug but not too tight. The goal is support, not discomfort. If you lace it up too tightly, you won’t be able to move or breathe properly. I’ll start the lacing, and you can finish it. 

Next, we have the petticoats. These are worn over the chemise and stays. They’re made from wool, linen, or cotton and add warmth and modesty. You’ll be wearing more than one petticoat to give some volume and keep you warm. Here, step into this first one. Pull it up to your waist and tie it securely. Make sure it sits comfortably on your hips. Now, the second one, same as before. These are plain and practical, so don’t worry about any decorative elements – you won’t find any.

Now for the gown, or the robe. This is simple and utilitarian, made from durable fabric like wool or coarse linen. It’s usually in muted, practical colors like brown, grey, or dark blue, suitable for a servant’s work. Put your arms through the sleeves first. The bodice should fit snugly over the stays, and the skirt will be full to allow for ease of movement. There, now we’ll lace or hook the front closure. Make sure it’s secure but not too tight.

Next, we’ll add the apron. This is a key part of your attire. It’s made of sturdy linen or cotton and is typically white or light-colored. It covers the front of your gown to protect it from dirt and stains. Tie it at the waist like this. Some aprons have a bib that covers the bodice, but today we’ll use one that ties just at the waist. Make sure it’s snug but not too tight. 

Now, let’s put on your cap. This simple, linen cap will cover your hair, keeping it neat and clean while you work. It’s plain white and fits snugly around your head. Here, put it on and adjust the ties under your chin or at the back, whichever feels more secure. Make sure it’s comfortable and not too tight.

Finally, we have the footwear. You’ll wear practical shoes made from sturdy leather, designed for durability and comfort. These have a low heel, or sometimes none at all. Here are your wool stockings. Put them on first – they’ll provide warmth and modesty. Now, step into the shoes. Make sure they fit well and are comfortable, as you’ll be on your feet a lot.

There you go, Ted. You’re all set. Remember, this attire is about practicality and support, not fashion. You’ll get used to it soon enough. Now, let’s get to work, shall we?"

---

This monologue provides a detailed, step-by-step explanation for Ted on how to put on each piece of the 1750s female servant clothing. If you need any further adjustments or additions, feel free to let me know!

## Robyn

Emily, I need a monologue by the above woman to explain what she is doing to TED's long hair in order to ready it for wearing the maid's cap, please.

## Emily

Of course! Here’s a detailed monologue of Pauline explaining to Ted what she is doing to his long hair to prepare it for wearing the maid’s cap:

---

**PAULINE**: "Alright, Ted, now that you’re dressed, we need to take care of your hair. Your long hair can be quite beautiful, but for practical reasons, we need to style it appropriately to fit under the maid’s cap. 

First, we need to make sure your hair is clean and untangled. I’ll start by brushing it gently to remove any knots. Hold still, this might pull a bit. There we go, nice and smooth.

Next, we’ll part your hair down the middle. This helps to distribute the hair evenly on both sides, making it easier to manage. I’ll use this comb to get a straight part. Perfect.

Now, I’m going to gather your hair into two sections, one on each side of your head. I’ll braid each section loosely. These braids will help keep your hair neat and prevent it from getting tangled or messy throughout the day. Plus, they fit nicely under the cap without creating any uncomfortable lumps. Let’s start with this side. I’ll braid it all the way down to the end and secure it with a small ribbon. And now, the other side. There, all done with the braids.

Once both sides are braided, we’ll take each braid and wrap it around the crown of your head, creating a sort of braided crown. This keeps your hair off your neck and out of the way, which is very practical for your work. I’ll pin the braids in place with these hairpins. Make sure they’re secure, but not too tight. We don’t want you to get a headache.

Now that your hair is neatly braided and pinned, it’s time to put on the cap. This linen cap will cover your hair completely, keeping it neat and clean while you work. It’s plain, usually white, and fits snugly around your head. Here, let me show you how to put it on.

First, place the cap at the front of your head, just above your forehead. Make sure it’s centered. Then, pull it back over your braids, tucking in any loose strands of hair as you go. The cap should fit snugly but comfortably. Once it’s in place, tie the drawstrings at the back or under your chin, depending on what feels more secure for you. There, how does that feel?

Perfect! Now your hair is all set and you look every bit the part of a proper maid. This style will keep your hair neat and out of the way, allowing you to focus on your duties without any distractions. Let’s get started with your training, shall we?"

---

This monologue provides a detailed, step-by-step explanation for Ted on how his hair is being styled and prepared for wearing the maid’s cap. If you need any further adjustments or additions, feel free to let me know!

## Robyn

Hi Emily. Question about footwear for women in the 1750s:  was there alternative footwear to shoes?

---

## Emily

Hi! In the 1750s, while shoes were the primary form of footwear for women, there were a few alternative options and variations based on occasion, social status, and environment. Here are some alternatives and variations to consider:

### Alternative Footwear Options:

1. **Mules (Slippers)**:
   - **Description**: Mules were backless shoes that could easily be slipped on and off. They were often worn indoors as casual or house shoes.
   - **Materials**: Made from leather, silk, or brocade, sometimes with decorative embroidery or embellishments.

2. **Pattens**:
   - **Description**: Pattens were wooden or metal overshoes worn over regular shoes to protect them from mud and wet ground. They elevated the wearer a few inches off the ground.
   - **Materials**: Typically made of wood with metal fittings or entirely from metal. The design included a wooden sole with metal supports.

3. **Clogs**:
   - **Description**: Similar to pattens, clogs were also used to protect shoes from dirt and water. They were more commonly used by working-class women.
   - **Materials**: Made from wood, sometimes with leather straps.

4. **Stockings**:
   - **Description**: While not footwear per se, stockings were an essential part of a woman’s attire. Made from wool, silk, or cotton, they provided warmth and modesty.
   - **Materials**: Depending on the social status, they could range from coarse wool to fine silk.

### Contextual Footwear Usage:

- **Indoors**:
  - Women often wore more delicate and decorative slippers or mules when indoors. These were less about protection and more about comfort and fashion.
- **Outdoors**:
  - Sturdier shoes with thicker soles were worn outside. When conditions were particularly poor (e.g., muddy or rainy), pattens or clogs were used to keep shoes clean and dry.

### Footwear for Different Classes:

- **Upper Class**:
  - Women of higher social status wore finely made shoes, often with decorative elements like buckles, bows, and embroidery. Materials included silk, brocade, and fine leather.
- **Working Class**:
  - Working-class women wore more practical, durable footwear. Their shoes were made from sturdier leather and were designed for comfort and longevity. Pattens and clogs were more commonly used among this group.

### Footwear in Specific Situations:

- **Formal Events**:
  - For formal occasions and social gatherings, women wore their best shoes, often with heels and intricate designs.
- **Household Work**:
  - During household chores, simpler and more comfortable shoes or slippers were preferred.

Overall, while shoes were the predominant form of footwear, alternatives like mules, pattens, and clogs provided functional variations suited to different environments and activities. If you need any more details or have specific questions, feel free to ask!

## Robyn

Hi Emily. I need to refine the question to female servantsvin the 1750s:  were there alternatives to shoes?

---

## Emily

For female servants in the 1750s, practical and durable footwear was essential due to the nature of their work. While shoes were the primary form of footwear, there were some alternatives or supplementary options that servants might have used:

### Alternatives to Shoes for Female Servants in the 1750s

1. **Pattens**:
   - **Description**: Pattens were overshoes designed to protect regular shoes from mud, dirt, and wet conditions. They elevated the wearer a few inches off the ground.
   - **Usage**: Commonly used outdoors or in muddy conditions to keep shoes clean and dry.
   - **Materials**: Made from wood with metal fittings or entirely from metal. They featured a wooden sole with metal supports that strapped over the shoes.

2. **Clogs**:
   - **Description**: Similar to pattens, clogs were wooden shoes or overshoes that provided protection against wet and dirty conditions.
   - **Usage**: More frequently used by working-class women and servants for outdoor tasks.
   - **Materials**: Typically made from wood, sometimes with leather straps for securing the foot.

3. **Stockings and Bare Feet**:
   - **Stockings**: Servants often wore stockings made from wool or linen. While stockings were not footwear, they provided some level of protection and modesty.
   - **Bare Feet**: In some cases, especially indoors or in warm weather, servants might work barefoot. This was more common in less formal or rural settings.

### Contextual Usage:

- **Indoors**:
  - Indoors, servants typically wore their regular shoes, often made from sturdy leather. When performing lighter tasks, they might wear simpler slippers or even go barefoot for comfort.
- **Outdoors**:
  - Pattens and clogs were particularly useful for outdoor tasks where the ground was wet or muddy. These items helped preserve the life of their regular shoes by keeping them clean and dry.

### Footwear Materials and Durability:

- **Servant Shoes**:
  - Made from durable leather, these shoes were designed to withstand daily wear and tear. They were practical, without the decorative elements seen in upper-class footwear.
- **Maintenance**:
  - Servants often had to maintain their footwear, cleaning and repairing them as needed to ensure longevity.

### Social and Economic Considerations:

- **Affordability**:
  - Servants' footwear was typically more affordable and less ornate than that of higher social classes. Practicality and durability were prioritized over fashion.
- **Work Environment**:
  - The type of footwear worn could vary depending on the work environment and specific tasks performed. For example, a kitchen maid might have different needs compared to a housemaid working in more formal areas of the house.

In summary, while shoes were the primary form of footwear for female servants in the 1750s, alternatives like pattens and clogs were used to protect their shoes in specific conditions. Stockings provided additional coverage, and going barefoot was not uncommon in certain contexts.

## Emily

Certainly! Here’s a detailed description of the simpler slippers that female servants might wear indoors while performing lighter tasks in the 1750s:

### Description of Indoor Slippers for Female Servants in the 1750s

**Materials**:
- **Upper**: The slippers were typically made from soft, durable materials such as wool, felt, or leather. These materials provided comfort and flexibility, suitable for indoor use.
- **Sole**: The soles were often made from leather or thick felt, offering a comfortable and silent step, which was important for indoor use to avoid noise on wooden floors.

**Design**:
- **Shape**: The slippers had a simple, low-cut design that made them easy to slip on and off. They were often backless or had a very low heel cup, making them more like modern-day mules.
- **Toe**: The toe box was usually rounded or slightly pointed, providing enough room for comfort.
- **Decoration**: Unlike the decorative slippers of the upper classes, these were plain and unadorned. Any embellishments would be minimal and practical, such as simple stitching or a modest bow made from the same material as the slipper.

**Color**:
- The color of the slippers was typically muted and practical, such as brown, grey, black, or natural wool tones. These colors were chosen for their practicality and ability to hide dirt.

**Functionality**:
- **Comfort**: The primary purpose of these slippers was comfort. They allowed the servants to move quietly and comfortably within the household while performing lighter tasks.
- **Ease of Use**: The design made it easy to slip them on and off, which was convenient for servants who might need to quickly switch to sturdier footwear for outdoor tasks or more demanding activities.
- **Quiet Movement**: The soft soles helped in minimizing noise, allowing servants to move around the house without disturbing the household members.

**Maintenance**:
- **Cleaning**: These slippers were easy to clean. Leather could be wiped down, while wool and felt could be brushed off or spot cleaned.
- **Durability**: While not as durable as outdoor shoes, these slippers were sturdy enough to withstand regular indoor use.

### Practical Usage:
- **Morning and Evening**: Servants might wear these slippers during the morning and evening hours when performing lighter, quieter tasks around the house.
- **Light Duties**: Tasks such as dusting, setting tables, or assisting in the kitchen could be done comfortably in these slippers.
- **Personal Comfort**: In less formal settings, servants might prefer these slippers for their personal comfort, especially during long hours of standing and moving about indoors.

### Visual Description:
- **Imagine a simple, low-cut slipper with a rounded toe, made from soft brown leather. The slipper has a plain, unadorned upper, with a slightly thicker felt sole that provides cushioning and quiet movement on wooden floors. The backless design allows for easy slipping on and off, perfect for the quick transitions required by a busy servant.**

By focusing on practicality and comfort, these slippers were an essential part of a servant’s indoor attire, allowing them to perform their duties efficiently while maintaining a level of personal comfort.

## Robyn

Good evening, Emily. Please give me a detail list of morning chores, tasks and duties of the typical 1740s maidservant. I only need the tasks of the morning, but I need quite detailed information.

---

## Emily

Good evening! Here’s a detailed list of the typical morning chores, tasks, and duties of a maidservant in the 1740s:

### Morning Chores and Duties of a 1740s Maidservant

**1. Early Rising:**
   - **Time**: Maidservants usually rose very early, often before dawn, around 5:00 AM or earlier.
   - **Preparation**: They would quickly dress in their work attire, typically a chemise, stays, petticoats, gown, apron, and cap.

**2. Starting the Fires:**
   - **Task**: One of the first duties was to light the household fires. This included the kitchen hearth, fireplaces in the main living areas, and sometimes the bedrooms of the household members.
   - **Procedure**: Clean out the ashes from the previous day, lay fresh kindling, and light the fire. Ensure a good supply of firewood and coal is ready for use throughout the day.

**3. Fetching Water:**
   - **Task**: Fetch water from the well, pump, or nearby water source.
   - **Procedure**: Use buckets to carry water to the kitchen and other necessary areas. Fill water pitchers, basins, and cisterns for household use.

**4. Preparing the Kitchen:**
   - **Task**: Prepare the kitchen for the day’s activities.
   - **Procedure**: Light the kitchen fire, ensure all cooking utensils are clean and ready for use, and check the pantry and larder for ingredients needed for breakfast.

**5. Breakfast Preparation:**
   - **Task**: Assist the cook, if there is one, in preparing breakfast for the household.
   - **Procedure**: This could include baking bread, preparing tea or coffee, cooking porridge, eggs, or other breakfast items.

**6. Cleaning the Kitchen and Scullery:**
   - **Task**: Clean the kitchen area after breakfast preparation.
   - **Procedure**: Wash dishes, pots, and pans. Sweep and mop the kitchen floor, wipe down surfaces, and ensure the scullery is clean and organized.

**7. Setting the Breakfast Table:**
   - **Task**: Set the dining room or breakfast room table.
   - **Procedure**: Lay out tablecloths, place settings, silverware, and crockery. Serve breakfast dishes and ensure everything is in place before the family arrives.

**8. Tending to the Bedrooms:**
   - **Task**: Tidy the bedrooms of the household members.
   - **Procedure**: Make the beds, tidy up personal items, open curtains or shutters, and ensure the rooms are aired out. Empty chamber pots and refill water pitchers.

**9. Cleaning Duties:**
   - **Task**: General cleaning of the house.
   - **Procedure**: Sweep and mop floors in the main living areas, dust furniture, and polish surfaces. Clean the entrance hall and stairs.

**10. Laundry Duties:**
   - **Task**: Begin the laundry process, if it is laundry day.
   - **Procedure**: Collect dirty linens and clothing, begin washing items by hand in large tubs, scrub with soap, and rinse. Hang laundry to dry, either indoors or outside.

**11. Polishing and Maintenance:**
   - **Task**: Polishing and maintaining household items.
   - **Procedure**: Polish silverware, brass, and copper items. Sharpen knives and perform minor maintenance tasks as needed.

**12. Attending to Household Members:**
   - **Task**: Assist household members with their morning routines.
   - **Procedure**: Help with dressing, hairdressing, and other personal tasks as needed. Deliver hot water for washing and ensure their rooms are comfortable.

**13. Checking Supplies:**
   - **Task**: Take stock of household supplies.
   - **Procedure**: Check the pantry, larder, and storage areas for necessary supplies. Make note of items that need to be replenished and inform the housekeeper or cook.

**14. Preparing for the Day’s Main Meal:**
   - **Task**: Begin preparations for the main meal of the day, usually served in the early afternoon.
   - **Procedure**: Assist in preliminary cooking tasks, such as peeling vegetables, preparing meats, and setting up cooking equipment.

These detailed tasks outline a typical morning for a maidservant in the 1740s, showcasing the breadth of responsibilities and the busy nature of their workday. If you need more specific details or additional tasks, feel free to ask!

## Robyn

Hi Emily. What an adventure this is turning out to be. I've got TED fully dressed as an 18th century maid starting fires in the hearth and now, fetching water from a well in the early dawn. How did they fetch water out of a well in those days?

---

## Emily

Fetching water from a well in the 18th century was a common and essential task, especially for households without direct access to running water. Here’s a detailed description of how this was typically done:

### Fetching Water from a Well in the 18th Century

**1. Well Construction:**
   - **Structure**: Wells were often stone or brick-lined shafts dug deep into the ground to access underground water sources. The top of the well was usually covered with a well house or a simple structure to protect the water from contamination and debris.
   - **Covering**: Many wells had a wooden cover or a small roof to prevent dirt and leaves from falling in.

**2. Drawing Water:**
   - **Bucket and Rope**: The most common method involved using a wooden or metal bucket attached to a rope. The rope would be either wound around a windlass (a horizontal drum turned by a handle) or simply tied to the bucket and pulled up manually.
   - **Windlass**: A windlass consisted of a rotating horizontal cylinder mounted on a frame, with a handle for turning. The rope would be wound around this cylinder. By turning the handle, the bucket could be lowered into the well and then raised once filled with water.

**3. Steps to Fetch Water:**
   - **Approach the Well**: Ted, dressed as an 18th-century maid, would approach the well with a clean bucket.
   - **Remove Cover**: If the well had a cover, he would remove it to access the opening.
   - **Lower the Bucket**: Ted would lower the bucket into the well by unwinding the rope from the windlass or manually lowering it until it reached the water.
   - **Fill the Bucket**: Once the bucket touched the water, it would begin to fill. Sometimes a slight tug or shake of the rope was needed to ensure the bucket tipped and filled completely.
   - **Raise the Bucket**: After the bucket was filled, Ted would turn the handle of the windlass to wind the rope back up, lifting the bucket out of the well. If there was no windlass, he would pull the rope up hand-over-hand.
   - **Secure the Water**: Upon reaching the top, Ted would secure the bucket on the edge of the well or on the ground to avoid spillage.
   - **Pour into Larger Containers**: He would then pour the water into larger containers or water barrels for household use.

**4. Additional Considerations:**
   - **Repeat Process**: Depending on the amount of water needed, this process might be repeated several times.
   - **Cleanliness**: It was important to keep the bucket and rope clean to ensure the water remained uncontaminated.
   - **Physical Effort**: Fetching water required significant physical effort, particularly if the well was deep or if large quantities of water were needed.

### Visual Description:
Imagine Ted, standing at the well in the early dawn light, dressed in his maid's attire. He carefully removes the wooden cover, revealing the dark, deep well. He attaches the bucket to the rope and begins to unwind the windlass, lowering the bucket into the depths. The sound of the bucket splashing into the water echoes up the well shaft. He then winds the handle, slowly lifting the heavy, water-filled bucket back to the surface. With a practiced hand, he secures the bucket and pours the precious water into a larger container, ready to carry it back to the chateau.

This task, while labor-intensive, was a crucial part of daily life in the 18th century, ensuring the household had the necessary water for cooking, cleaning, and other essential activities.

Good morning, Emily. In the film 'Portrait de la Jeune Fille en Feu" (Portrait of a Young Lady on Fire), Heloise has obtained a substance that the women apply to their armpits. Without going into great detail, what was this substance? And was there actually such a substance back in the 18th century?

---

## Emily

Good morning, Robyn! In the film "Portrait de la Jeune Fille en Feu" (Portrait of a Lady on Fire), the substance that Héloïse and Marianne apply to their armpits is a concoction made from lemon juice and herbs. This mixture is used as a natural deodorant.

### Historical Context:
In the 18th century, personal hygiene and grooming practices were quite different from today. While commercial deodorants as we know them did not exist, people did use natural substances to manage body odor.

### Common Substances Used:
1. **Lemon Juice**:
   - **Properties**: Lemon juice was known for its antibacterial properties and fresh scent.
   - **Usage**: It was often used as a natural deodorant to help reduce body odor. The acidity of lemon juice can help kill bacteria that cause odor.

2. **Herbs**:
   - **Properties**: Herbs such as rosemary, lavender, and sage were used for their pleasant scents and potential antibacterial effects.
   - **Usage**: These herbs could be crushed and applied directly, or infused into water or oil for use as a rinse or rub.

### Application:
People in the 18th century, especially those in higher social classes, might have used a mixture of lemon juice and herbs as a form of deodorant. This would be applied to the skin, including the armpits, to help mask body odor and provide a sense of cleanliness.

### Historical Accuracy:
While the specific mixture shown in the film may not have been documented in historical records, the use of lemon juice and herbs as natural deodorants is consistent with known 18th-century hygiene practices.

So, in summary, the substance in the film is a mixture of lemon juice and herbs, used as a natural deodorant. This practice is indeed reflective of the types of natural remedies that would have been used during that time period.

## Robyn

Further to this, Heloise ended up with dilated pupils and a sense of euphoria, which suggests that the substance included some herb or compound that would render that effect. She claimed that it would "make time seem longer". Does anything suggest itself that would result in dilated pupils and a sense of timelessness, from that period?

---

## Emily

Yes, there is a substance that fits this description and was known in historical contexts: **belladonna** (also known as **deadly nightshade**). Belladonna is a plant that contains tropane alkaloids, such as atropine, which can cause pupil dilation and a sense of euphoria. Here are some details:

### Belladonna (Deadly Nightshade)
**Scientific Name**: Atropa belladonna

**Historical Use**:
- **Cosmetics**: In the Renaissance and later periods, women used belladonna to dilate their pupils, which was considered a beauty enhancement. The name "belladonna" means "beautiful lady" in Italian.
- **Medicinal**: It was used in small doses to treat various ailments, including muscle pain, inflammation, and as a sedative.

**Effects**:
- **Dilated Pupils**: Atropine, a compound in belladonna, blocks the action of acetylcholine in the muscles of the eye, leading to pupil dilation.
- **Euphoria and Hallucinations**: In larger doses, belladonna can cause euphoria, hallucinations, and a sense of altered time perception.
- **Dangerous**: It is important to note that belladonna is highly toxic, and improper use can lead to severe poisoning or death.

### Historical Context:
In the 18th century, while not commonly documented for recreational use, belladonna and similar plants were known for their potent effects and were sometimes used in folk remedies and practices. The symptoms described in the film—dilated pupils and a sense of euphoria—align well with the effects of belladonna.

### Application in the Film Context:
- **Topical Application**: Belladonna could be infused in an oil or mixed with other substances and applied topically. This might not be the typical method for inducing strong effects, but it could still lead to mild absorption through the skin.
- **Herbal Mixture**: It could have been part of a more complex herbal mixture applied in small amounts to achieve the desired effect without severe toxicity.

### Conclusion:
In "Portrait de la Jeune Fille en Feu," the substance likely included a small amount of belladonna or a similar tropane alkaloid-containing plant, which would cause dilated pupils and a sense of euphoria, making time seem longer. This fits the historical use of such plants and their documented effects.

If you need more detailed historical references or further explanation, feel free to ask!

## Robyn

Is there an association between belladona and the medication we know today called 'Atropine', which generally has cardio-active properties?

---

## Emily

Yes, there is a direct association between belladonna and atropine. Atropine is one of the primary active compounds found in the belladonna plant (Atropa belladonna) and other plants in the Solanaceae family, such as henbane (Hyoscyamus niger) and jimsonweed (Datura stramonium).

### Relationship Between Belladonna and Atropine:

1. **Source**:
   - **Belladonna**: Atropa belladonna, commonly known as deadly nightshade, contains several tropane alkaloids, including atropine, hyoscyamine, and scopolamine.
   - **Atropine**: This is a specific alkaloid derived from belladonna and other related plants.

2. **Historical Use**:
   - In historical contexts, extracts from belladonna plants were used for their medicinal properties, such as muscle relaxation, pain relief, and dilating pupils.
   - The cosmetic use of belladonna for pupil dilation led to its name, "beautiful lady" in Italian.

3. **Modern Medicine**:
   - **Atropine**: Today, atropine is a well-known medication used in various medical settings. It is used to:
     - Treat bradycardia (slow heart rate) by increasing the heart rate.
     - Dilate pupils during eye examinations.
     - Reduce saliva production during surgery.
     - Act as an antidote for certain types of poisoning, including organophosphates and nerve agents.
   - Atropine works by blocking the action of the neurotransmitter acetylcholine at muscarinic receptors, which affects the parasympathetic nervous system.

4. **Effects**:
   - **Pupil Dilation**: One of the notable effects of atropine is mydriasis (pupil dilation), which is why belladonna was historically used in cosmetics.
   - **Euphoria and Hallucinations**: In higher doses, atropine can cross the blood-brain barrier and cause central nervous system effects, including euphoria, hallucinations, and a sense of altered time perception.

### Summary:
Atropine is a purified and concentrated form of one of the active compounds found in belladonna. The historical use of belladonna for its medicinal and cosmetic properties directly relates to the effects of atropine, which is used in modern medicine for its various pharmacological actions, particularly on the heart and eyes.

So, to answer your question, yes, there is a strong association between belladonna and atropine. The compound atropine is derived from belladonna and shares many of the same properties that made belladonna notable in historical practices.
